<div id="review_1126554363" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1126554363" />
    <a title="Craig" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4760634-craig"><img alt="Craig" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577908463p2/4760634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1126554363?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Craig" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Craig" href="/user/show/4760634-craig">Craig</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Library Binding 9781439501696" href="/book/show/9650332-lilith-s-brood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4760634-craig?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1126554363&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1126554363" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1200153276943505803">"You are horror and beauty in rare combination."<br/><br/>Claustrophobic and taut, Butler knows how to play out the psychological effects of the mundane as well as the fantastic. And just when you start to think, "gee, these reactions are pretty tame," the real horrors start to be uncovered: genetic tampering, psychological edits/ rewrites, and slavery/ imprisonment, the real underpinning being "Who will win this Space Race?"<br/><br/>Look, Butler is not going to let the reader out of this conundrum with easy answe</span>
  <span id="freeText1200153276943505803" style="display:none">"You are horror and beauty in rare combination."<br /><br />Claustrophobic and taut, Butler knows how to play out the psychological effects of the mundane as well as the fantastic. And just when you start to think, "gee, these reactions are pretty tame," the real horrors start to be uncovered: genetic tampering, psychological edits/ rewrites, and slavery/ imprisonment, the real underpinning being "Who will win this Space Race?"<br /><br />Look, Butler is not going to let the reader out of this conundrum with easy answers: for example, I was very disturbed by Lilith's "no rape!" policy only applying to women. When she allows a man -- her partner -- to be raped (not "seduced") by the aliens (and this won't be the last time this happens either), I was immediately questioning her autonomy. Are they puppets of the aliens, or are they really agreeing to being treated like animals, simply to survive and get back to Earth? Even still, this rape left me more than a little rattled (and gender has no bearing on my rape aversion, fyi. Rape is rape, no matter the victim's gender). And perhaps that's what Butler wants her male readers to feel so that they have a better sense of what rape is. But the notion that Lilith convinces herself that Joseph is enjoying it is incredibly disturbing, especially when he repeatedly says "no".<br /><br />In the second book, Akin, Lilith's son, becomes the potential savior of humanity. I expected more here, I have to confess, because the possibilities for resolution seemed very clear to me: Akin could simply merge with the other ooloi and steal the secret for unlocking human reproduction or infecting them with his own humanity to plant the seed for showing why humans need to be free, putting him in the position of being a traitor to his own people (echoing the story of Lilith in the first book). But we didn't get that. It's a lot of talking and made-up alien words (which I just skimmed over) and humans killing other humans. Damn, if Butler isn't a fatalist. It makes you wonder what happened to her in life that her view of humanity is so dark. Not that I disagree with her, for the most part, but it can get more than a little tedious reading lectures from the aliens about how we suck.<br /><br />The last book drags and drones.<br /><br />-1 star for rapey-ness and for a really bad romance novel/ mammogram brochure cover</span>
  <a data-text-id="1200153276943505803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_9014248" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9014248" />
    <a title="Elise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/616282-elise"><img alt="Elise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245861216p2/616282.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9014248?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elise" href="/user/show/616282-elise">Elise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616282-elise?shelf=sci-fi-fantasy">sci-fi-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9014248" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2627273120845961494">Octavia Butler uses this book to explore what makes us human by taking humanity completely out of its known context and giving it a whole new one with fundamental restrictions and specifically chosen opportunities. This allows her to put humanity in high-relief, and I have to say a lot of what she says strikes a chord -- her definition of the Human Contradiction, for example, is spot-on. I think she does get a little bit heavy-handed -- I feel there's a little more gray scale to human behavior t</span>
  <span id="freeText2627273120845961494" style="display:none">Octavia Butler uses this book to explore what makes us human by taking humanity completely out of its known context and giving it a whole new one with fundamental restrictions and specifically chosen opportunities. This allows her to put humanity in high-relief, and I have to say a lot of what she says strikes a chord -- her definition of the Human Contradiction, for example, is spot-on. I think she does get a little bit heavy-handed -- I feel there's a little more gray scale to human behavior than perhaps she decided to paint on -- but all in all it's an amazing story and one which taught me a little bit more about what it means to be human. I don't mean to make it sound pedantic -- there's plenty of plot and character development to keep you hooked from page one. There was only one part of the first book where I started to lose steam early on, and then it gets <i>really</i> interesting from there on out. <br />I will also add that I am not a big huge fan of sci-fi that creates too much new lingo and too many new characters, classes, races, etc. I think Lilith's Brood keeps a good balance of giving just enough of said newness to keep you intrigued and immersed without creating a need for a dictionary of terms and characters built into the back! </span>
  <a data-text-id="2627273120845961494" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer652035189" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11281893462944011858">It's been a few months since I read this, but I realized I'd not reviewed it and wanted to put in a few words.<br/><br/>I can't express to you how refreshing it was to read an African American female protagonist who didn't speak with urban slang, who wasn't worried about finding a man and, in general, didn't fit the tired stereotypes that a lot of modern authors (both White and Non-White) tend to force Black characters into. <br/><br/>Dawn starts off in an intentionally confusing and intense way. Lilith wakes up in</span>
  <span id="freeText11281893462944011858" style="display:none">It's been a few months since I read this, but I realized I'd not reviewed it and wanted to put in a few words.<br /><br />I can't express to you how refreshing it was to read an African American female protagonist who didn't speak with urban slang, who wasn't worried about finding a man and, in general, didn't fit the tired stereotypes that a lot of modern authors (both White and Non-White) tend to force Black characters into. <br /><br />Dawn starts off in an intentionally confusing and intense way. Lilith wakes up in a small room with no way out and no indication of how long she's been there. Periodically she's put to sleep, with the gut feeling that "something" is being done to her while she's out.<br /><br />Eventually, she's introduced to one of her "captors." (I use quotations, because, well... it's complicated.) They'd like her to represent them and act as their go-between as they "wake up" more of Lilith's fellow humans.<br /><br />I don't want to go too far into this, because while Lilith's task is basically simple... it's really not and you'll have to read the book to learn more. <br /><br />I will say that Ms Butler does a fantastic job of expressing not just Lilith's ambiguity towards her task, but also her anger towards the Oankali and the humans. The tension Lilith feels is clearly mirrored by the humans until eventually things fall apart. And, even though I was expecting it (Ms Butler had successfully created a slow burn that built over the course of a couple of chapters), I was still saddened and disappointed by it.<br /><br />Skip forward a bit to Adulthood Rites, and it starts to become clear just how <i>different</i> the Oankali (and the new constructs) are from humans. Akin tries to find his place (ironically, the exact same problem most kids his age have) being half human, half Oankali on a planet that has been ripped apart by humans and the rebuilt into something strange and new by the Oankali. There's still tension between the humans and Oankali, and, for some reason, the Oankali decide that it's up to Akin to fix it for them.<br /><br />I honestly don't remember much from this one, other than that I found Akin utterly adorable.<br /><br />I don't really know how to describe Imago. Jodahs, the first neuter construct, has a hard time of it. There were moments when I just couldn't figure him/her/it out. I wasn't as captured by Jodahs as I was by Lilith and Akin. Imago, I think, was the least fulfilling of the series, but still worth the time.<br /><br />It should be noted that there are numerous scenes of coerced sex throughout the series. I don't know if it can be classified as rape, but it made me uncomfortable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11281893462944011858" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2253648-hazel?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53837694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5920749861456307288">Sterling; Butler died too soon.<br/><br/>This is thoughtful, intelligent science fiction, with interstellar travel, but nary a blaster in sight. I think I'd still consider this <i>hard sci-fi</i>. Butler must have done a great deal of research into genetics, biochemistry, and neurochemistry. She clearly knew a lot about human psychology too.<br/><br/>Her writing is spare, and tight, with few extraneous words/ descriptions. Nevertheless, the world and the experiences she describes are immediate and highly sensual. On this </span>
  <span id="freeText5920749861456307288" style="display:none">Sterling; Butler died too soon.<br /><br />This is thoughtful, intelligent science fiction, with interstellar travel, but nary a blaster in sight. I think I'd still consider this <i>hard sci-fi</i>. Butler must have done a great deal of research into genetics, biochemistry, and neurochemistry. She clearly knew a lot about human psychology too.<br /><br />Her writing is spare, and tight, with few extraneous words/ descriptions. Nevertheless, the world and the experiences she describes are immediate and highly sensual. On this reading I'm struck by all the subtle eroticism. I think Butler was very aware of how much we are governed by our biology, in particular our biochemistry. We tend to forget that. She's also aware of how much we lie to ourselves, or refuse to see unpalatable truths. Perhaps most striking to me, she reminds us how dangerous we can be, when we act out of fear and ignorance.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1671137200" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Dave" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5896715-dave"><img alt="Dave" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311785874p2/5896715.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer6429458793191994418">This book is mind-blowing. Lilith wakes up from a nuclear war to find she, and all other humans, have been scooped up from a dying earth, frozen for long enough for the Earth to recover, and are now being awakened to be mated with and returned to Earth to populate it with alien, human hybrids. Classic B grade sci-fi stuff right? Wrong! <br/><br/>From that fodder comes a story of incredible complexity and existential horror, as the story's focus shifts through the generations of Lillith's own hybrid childr</span>
  <span id="freeText6429458793191994418" style="display:none">This book is mind-blowing. Lilith wakes up from a nuclear war to find she, and all other humans, have been scooped up from a dying earth, frozen for long enough for the Earth to recover, and are now being awakened to be mated with and returned to Earth to populate it with alien, human hybrids. Classic B grade sci-fi stuff right? Wrong! <br /><br />From that fodder comes a story of incredible complexity and existential horror, as the story's focus shifts through the generations of Lillith's own hybrid children as they grow, find mates. Their behaviours are so incredibly alien, and viewed by humans, utterly horrific, but to you the reader now seem so normal and right. Your sympathies are always with the aliens and never with the humans. <br /><br />This is simply the best and most original sci-fi I have read this year so far. <br /></span>

<div id="review_2753437468" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2753437468" />
    <a title="Jo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23043313-jo"><img alt="Jo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555585027p2/23043313.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2753437468?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jo" href="/user/show/23043313-jo">Jo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23043313-jo?shelf=borrowed">borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23043313-jo?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23043313-jo?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2753437468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10167732262407366018">⭐ The Oankali are without a doubt in my top five aliens. They are superfluously Other; I was impressed throughout the trilogy by how sinister and inhuman Butler made them. She does that in many different ways, too - books two and three expand greatly on Oankali abilities, history and motivations and I was just constantly disturbed and delighted by how utterly weird they are.<br/><br/>⭐ It's the most subtle and thought-provoking depiction of Colonialism I've yet come across in science fiction, and I think </span>
  <span id="freeText10167732262407366018" style="display:none">⭐ The Oankali are without a doubt in my top five aliens. They are superfluously Other; I was impressed throughout the trilogy by how sinister and inhuman Butler made them. She does that in many different ways, too - books two and three expand greatly on Oankali abilities, history and motivations and I was just constantly disturbed and delighted by how utterly weird they are.<br /><br />⭐ It's the most subtle and thought-provoking depiction of Colonialism I've yet come across in science fiction, and I think it moves way beyond that in books two and three. It made me question pretty much every moral I have, and gave no clear answers. That kind of self-interrogation is tiring but worthwhile; I rarely mull over/talk endlessly about books the way I did these three. I made notes. It was wild.<br /><br />⭐ Butler writes in a completely compelling way - eventually. I really disliked Dawn the first time I read it; slogged through it, even, but didn't stop thinking about it and went so far as to inflict it on others so I could talk to them about it (apologies again to the #bukekhleb folks). This time, I still didn't like Dawn, but I carried on a few pages into the second book and then I could not stop. Somehow, Butler made this trilogy so cohesive that I don't hate the first book so much anymore; it feels necessary to the whole.<br /><br />⭐ Just going to reiterate that the Oankali are really, really good aliens. They only get stranger as the trilogy goes on and I was horrified and delighted by them in basically equal measure. They're also villains who consider themselves saviours; that isn't done this well in anything else I've read.<br /><br />The downers:<br /><br />You do have to get through Dawn to get to the amazing parts. It isn't especially indicative of the other two.<br /><br />It's another one of those post-apocalyptic settings where apparently only arseholes survived; all of the humans are terrible in some way and most of them are REALLY terrible. Mind you, this pandemic is making me ever more of a misanthrope, so perhaps I've been too easy on Humanity and actually this trope has it right.<br /><br />There are only heterosexual, cis humans. This feels like a massive oversight and I was very disappointed by it.<br /><br />The biggest thing, for me: the pervasive sexualisation of children, by humans and by Oankali. I understand that this is an effective way to upset people and make monsters but it's so insidiously, quietly done; difficult to discuss without spoilers but it comes up in Fledgling as well and I just don't like it.<br /><br />This trilogy, taken as a whole, is incredible, but I didn't enjoy it in the traditional sense. And I'll never be able to look at sea anemones without cringing again. I'd still recommend it - read it and then come talk to me 😉<br /><br />Tl;dr: thought-and-discussion provoking; the aliens are awesome but everyone is awful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10167732262407366018" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1864966772" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1864966772" />
    <a title="Melissa Espiritu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42694048-melissa-espiritu"><img alt="Melissa Espiritu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539061155p2/42694048.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1864966772?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa Espiritu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa Espiritu" href="/user/show/42694048-melissa-espiritu">Melissa Espiritu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1864966772" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15641505498075628470">If I could give half points, I would give this a 3.5. I do like the trilogy, but I wish I would have loved it more. The book definitely presents mind blowing concepts. And I have to hand it to her, Octavia Butler is the genius everyone says she is. The concept of the Oankali is the most unique fictional alien creation I have ever seen or read. <br/><br/> I really enjoyed much of the first book. How she presents Lilith's awakening, this alien world, and the plans to reinhabit Earth is fascinating. Lilith's</span>
  <span id="freeText15641505498075628470" style="display:none">If I could give half points, I would give this a 3.5. I do like the trilogy, but I wish I would have loved it more. The book definitely presents mind blowing concepts. And I have to hand it to her, Octavia Butler is the genius everyone says she is. The concept of the Oankali is the most unique fictional alien creation I have ever seen or read. <br /><br /> I really enjoyed much of the first book. How she presents Lilith's awakening, this alien world, and the plans to reinhabit Earth is fascinating. Lilith's character was so complex, but so very human. My biggest complaint about the trilogy is that the book doesn't continue to center Lilith in book two and three. It is a multigenerational saga. I get it, but Lilith's internal conflict was so powerful. I would have really enjoyed reading how it played out as she had construct children and as she became this legendary, misunderstood Mother to all of humanity. <br /><br /> Speaking of humanity, Butler's critique of humanity is spot on. I just wasn't sure what she was trying to say with the Oankali. Like I mentioned, some of the concepts she introduced with the Oankali really challenge convention, which I appreciate. The Oankali do not have a central leadership. They work with nature and reach consensus. They have a third gender which is NOT a combination of male and female, but just a whole other gender. The family structure really pushes our understanding of what defines a family. In so many ways, they were a superior species. But I was really disturbed by the ooloi and their insatiable sexuality. And I was disturbed by the issues of consent. There was a lot of "your mouth says no, but your body says yes" logic coming from the ooloi and that REALLY bothered me. The consent issues did not just come in the form of sexual activity, but also in how they sterilized all humans who refused to mate. I mean this mirrors a lot of what has been done to indigenous people and people of color in the Americas. Throughout the book, I could never tell what Butler was really trying to say with that, if in fact she critiqued it at all. Maybe she was trying to say all living species are problematic. Maybe I just need help with the analysis. <br /><br /> In the end, I just had a harder time relating to Lilith's construct children, Akin and Jodahs, who became central figures in book two and three. I had a hard time relating to the Oankali in general. They were just too alien for me, which I guess is to Butler's credit for creating a truly alien species. <br /><br />It was no Kindred, but if you're deep into Sci-Fi, then this book is okay.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15641505498075628470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1631168095" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1631168095" />
    <a title="Natalie Cannon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43815301-natalie-cannon"><img alt="Natalie Cannon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445612376p2/43815301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1631168095?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie Cannon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie Cannon" href="/user/show/43815301-natalie-cannon">Natalie Cannon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1631168095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11643559241163799152">There's not much I can add to the glowing pile of praise and accolades LILITH'S BROOD rightfully earns. Butler is simply the master of world-building. She's the mountains modern sci-fi is built on and she built her mountain out of concepts and concerns still relevant today: gender, race, and reproductive politics. <br/><br/>While I recognize that Butler is an amazing writer and I'm still giving LILITH'S BROOD 5 stars, the read wasn't all daisy chains for me. While the Oakanli keep presenting themselves as</span>
  <span id="freeText11643559241163799152" style="display:none">There's not much I can add to the glowing pile of praise and accolades LILITH'S BROOD rightfully earns. Butler is simply the master of world-building. She's the mountains modern sci-fi is built on and she built her mountain out of concepts and concerns still relevant today: gender, race, and reproductive politics. <br /><br />While I recognize that Butler is an amazing writer and I'm still giving LILITH'S BROOD 5 stars, the read wasn't all daisy chains for me. While the Oakanli keep presenting themselves as logical, they use a lot of sketchy manipulations to corner the humans into cooperating. Their seductions are cringe-worthy since the Oakanli go by what the humans' body is "saying" and not what the human is verbally telling them. The humans' consent is not explicit and continuing. At one point, Lilith is impregnated without her knowledge. While the Oakanli are somewhat punished for these actions, it keeps happening over and over. It's...squick-worthy. There's also a frick-ton of ableist language, genetic determinism, and a weird lack of humans on the LGBT spectrum that is not examined or contradicted.<br /><br />BUT BUT BUT nonbinary aliens! Neutral pronouns! Tentacles! Earth rehabilitation after nuclear holocaust! Lots of environmentalism! Mars colonies! Badass women! Badass women of color! (Lots and lots of people of color, actually). All the jerks die! Semi-sentient plants/spaceships! Read it, read it, and read it again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11643559241163799152" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('buTD84dS1X6leD7PO5wLVYQtod0LjBkBFw+FjR+EZN112pKqfeWvkN+BMkUJWdvR2JqztohVBONwRh7pWdan3w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KT2kGbqW19rueatV5O+QioC03mOVJIK1RNco7P6AlmEyA/VAQCGtNJSAp9/WKkAO3APMCBb9n1cjnrOIuNJVYw==')})
  }

Upon this tactical foundation, Drake uses historical metaphor to provide a rich and de<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15964800190350877791\" style=\"display:none\">With a veteran ’s eye for the harsh and gritty details of war, David Drake depicts a futuristic analog of Vietnam-era tank combat in his Hammer’s Slammers fiction. Upon this tactical foundation, Drake uses historical metaphor to provide a rich and detailed future-history that is both unique and strangely familiar. The characters that make up the Hammer’s Regiment are neither cartoon heroes nor propaganda villains; rather they are competent professionals engaged in a deadly business. The inevitable conflicts between policy, necessity, and human nature are often at the forefront of the Slammers narratives, and it is this rich fusion of elements that makes Drake’s Slammers fiction instantly identifiable and utterly compelling.  The Hammer’s Slammers series is some of the most important and influential work in military SF. The Vietnam War made indelible marks on the science fiction genre, and Drake’s service with the Blackhorse Regiment during the war gives his fiction a unique perspective. It is this perspective that is sometimes lacking in military SF, and one that makes Drake’s work so important to the science fiction genre as a whole. This three-volume set collects all the Slammers short fiction and novels into a set of uniform editions, and presents a new, previously uncollected piece of fiction in each volume.  This second volume collects the first four Slammers novels, At Any Price (1985), Counting the Cost (1987), Rolling Hot (1989) and The Warrior (1991). The novella “A Day of Glory” was written for this volume, and has been uncollected prior to the publication of tihs volume.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15964800190350877791\" href=\"#\"           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/29535.Octavia_E_Butler"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1242244143p3/29535.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/29535.Octavia_E_Butler">Octavia E. Butler
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
9,573
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18062243466221968825">Octavia Estelle Butler was an American science fiction writer, one of the best-known among the few African-American women in the field. She won both Hugo and Nebula awards. In 1995, she became the first science fiction writer to receive the MacArthur Foundation "Genius" Grant.</span>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/3739-lilith-s-brood-xenogenesis-books-1-3">Quotes from Lilith&#39;s Brood</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Intelligence is relatively new to life on Earth, but your hierarchical tendencies are ancient.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/7617847-intelligence-is-relatively-new-to-life-on-earth-but-your">7 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Human beings fear difference,” Lilith had told him once. “Oankali crave difference. Humans persecute their different ones, yet they need them to give themselves definition and status. Oankali seek difference and collect it. They need it to keep themselves from stagnation and overspecialization. If you don’t understand this, you will. You’ll probably find both tendencies surfacing in your own behavior.” And she had put her hand on his hair. “When you feel a conflict, try to go the Oankali way. Embrace difference.” Akin&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
